The Gods
Brhaspati Ã• ƒÄ·πÖgirasa, Brhaspati Laukya, or Aditi D Ã• ƒÅk·π£ƒÅya·πáƒ´
9 verses: anu·π£·π≠ubh
Geldner remarks that the hymn almost seems as if the poet intentionally wanted to show the insolubility of the problem of the origin of things by setting incompat ible theories of cosmogony alongside one another. While we find greater coherence than this, we recognize the complexities and uncertainties that caused Geldner to come to this conclusion. At least at a very general level, the hymn does show a cos mogonic progression from ‚Äúwhat is not existing‚Äù to ‚Äúwhat is existing‚Äù and finally to immortal gods and mortal humans.
The hymn also has thematic elements in common with the preceding hymn. The most obvious is that braÃÅhma·πáas paÃÅti·∏•, the Lord of the Sacred Formulation (vs. 2), is BrÃ•haspati, who is invoked at the beginning of X.71. Then too, while it has the appearance of a cosmogony, the hymn also makes repeated reference to sacrificial performance. One thematic strand of this hymn is that the sacrifice embodies the principles that gave rise to the world.
In verse 3 what BrÃ•haspati ‚Äúsmelted‚Äù is the ‚Äúbirths‚Äù of the gods, as SƒÅya·πáa already noted. BrÃ•haspati brings them about by means of a sacred formulation (braÃÅhman) expressing the origins of the gods. But such a formulation can also bring about the birth of the gods in a specific ritual place at the time of a ritual performance. In that way, words‚Äîor the thoughts behind words‚Äîcan make beings real. Perhaps this is what the poet means when in verse 2d he says that ‚Äúwhat exists,‚Äù what are ‚Äúthings‚Äù (the world, the gods, and humans), was born from ‚Äúwhat does not exist,‚Äù what are not ‚Äúthings‚Äù (thoughts or words).
In verse 3cd ‚Äúthe one whose feet were opened up‚Äù gives rise to what exists, which in turn gives rise to the regions of space. But in 4ab ‚Äúthe one whose feet were opened up‚Äù gives rise to the earth, which gives rise to the regions. That is to say, between the two hemistichs, there is a shift in the middle term: ‚Äúwhat exists‚Äù in verse 3 and ‚Äúthe earth‚Äù in verse 4. The key to the significance of this shift is the word bh≈´ÃÅ
‚Äúthe earth.‚Äù ‚ÄúThe earth‚Äù is only one possible translation; the word could also mean a ‚Äúform‚Äù that something assumes (cf. III.55.13b=X.27.14d) or, etymologically at least, the act of ‚Äúbecoming.‚Äù If the ambiguity is intentional, then the two verses together redefine ‚Äúwhat exists‚Äù as that which has material substance‚Äîmost broadly the earth or more specifically what has form.
The source of ‚Äúwhat exists‚Äù or ‚Äúthe earth‚Äù is the principle or entity described as ‚Äúone whose feet were opened up‚Äù (uttƒÅnaÃÅpad). Falk (1994: 7‚Äì10) understands uttƒÅnaÃÅ to describe something whose feet are ‚Äúturned upward,‚Äù perhaps the upside-down world tree with its roots above. We take the more common approach in seeing here an image of birth or possibly sexual availability. But then whose feet are these? If the answer is inside the hymn, then it might be the goddess Aditi, who appears in verse 4 and is a mother of the gods in verses 8‚Äì9. Or again, since ‚Äúwhat exists‚Äù (saÃÅt) is said to have arisen from ‚Äúwhat does not exist‚Äù (aÃÅsat) in verses 2 and 3, there is per
haps an implied identification of ‚Äúthe one whose feet were opened up‚Äù with ‚Äúwhat does not exist.‚Äù Whoever or whatever the uttƒÅnaÃÅpad may be, the term also contains an oblique reference to the lower araÃÅ·πáƒ´, one of the pieces of wood used to kindle the sacrificial fire. See, for example, III.29.3ab addressing the priests, uttƒÅnƒÅÃÅyƒÅm √°va bharƒÅ cikitvƒÅÃÅn, sady√°·∏• pr√°vƒ´tƒÅ vrÃ•ÃÅ·π£a·πáa·πÉ jajƒÅna ‚ÄúAttentive, bear down upon her who is opened up (uttƒÅnƒÅÃÅ) [=the lower fire-churning stick]. Impregnated on this day, she has given birth to the bull [=Agni].‚Äù That is to say, the appearance of fire from the fire-churning wood replicates the emergence of ‚Äúwhat exists‚Äù from ‚Äúwhat does not exist.‚Äù
In verse 4cd the poet describes the paradoxical birth of Aditi from Dak·π£a and Dak·π£a from Aditi. Aditi is not only a goddess, but also a principle, the principle of Innocence or Offenselessness against the gods. Likewise, Dak·π£a can be a god per sonifying ritual Skill. Their birth from one another may signify the mutual depen dence of freedom from offense against the gods and ritual skill, both of which are necessary to carry out the sacrifice. Similarly, there may also be reference to the sac rifice in verse 5d, in which the gods are said to be amrÃ•ÃÅtabandhava·∏•. The compound can mean that the gods are kin of the ‚Äúimmortal‚Äù Soma or Agni or that they are kin ‚Äúto immortality‚Äù or that they are connected to the immortal waters (Falk 1994: 13). There are possible references to the ritual or ritual mythology in the succeeding

verses as well. Thus, in 6d tƒ´vraÃÅ, ‚Äúsharp‚Äù or ‚Äúbitter,‚Äù is a typical description of soma. Or again in 7a the Yatis are ritual priests, whose death resulted in the rains that swell ‚Äúthe living worlds‚Äù (cf. Jamison 1991: 111). And finally in 7cd the sun in the waters can be a figure describing soma (cf. Oberlies 1999: 164‚Äì65) or fire.
The last two verses describe the birth of immortals, Aditi‚Äôs ‚Äúseven sons,‚Äù with whom she went to the ancient generation of gods (vs. 9ab), and the birth of mortals, who are born from a ‚Äúdead egg‚Äù (Hoffmann 1976b [=1992: 723]), a divine miscar riage, both ‚Äúfor procreation‚Äù and ‚Äúfor death‚Äù (9cd).
As a cosmology the hymn invites comparison with X.129.

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúLet us proclaim with a clear voice the genitive rations of the gods (the divine company), who, when theirpraises are recited, look (favourably on the worshipper) in this latter age.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

Now amid acclaim we will proclaim the births of the gods,
so that one in a later generation will see (them) as the hymns are recited.

Griffith

LET US with tuneful skill proclaim these generations of the Gods,
That one may see them when these hymns are chanted in a future age.

Geldner

Elizarenkova

English translation:

‚ÄúBrahma·πáaspati filled these (genitive rations of the gods) with breath as a blacksmith (his bellows); in thefirst age of the gods the existent was born of the non-existent.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

The Lord of the Sacred Formulation [=BrÃ•haspati] smelted these (births) like a smith.
In the ancient generation of the gods, what exists was born from what does not exist.

Griffith

These Brahmanaspati produced with blast and smelting, like a Smith,
Existence, in an earlier age of Gods, from Non-existence sprang.

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúIn the firsst age of the gods the existent was born of the non- existent; after that the quarters (of thehorizon) were born, and after them the upward-growing (trees).‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

In the first generation of the gods, what exists was born from what does not exist.
The regions of space were born following that (which exists)‚Äîthat
(which exists) was born from the one whose feet were opened up.

Griffith

Existence, in the earliest age of Gods, from Non-existence sprang.
Thereafter were the regions born. This sprang from the Productive Power.

Geldner

Im ersten Zeitalter der G√∂tter entstand das Seiende aus dem Nichts; nach diesem entstanden die Weltr√§ume: Dieses wurde von der Kauernden geboren.

Grassmann

Aus dem Nichtseienden entsprang zur ersten G√∂tterzeit das Sein; Der Welten R√§ume gingen dann hervor aus der Geb√§rerin,

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúThe earth was born from the upward-growing (tree), the quarters were born from the earth; Dak·π£awas born from Aditi, and afterwards Aditi from Dak·π£a.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

SƒÅy·πáa remarks: It may be objected that there is acontradiction here, since a self-produced effect cannot be the cause of itself. The venerable YƒÅska removingthis objection has questioned and refuted the inconsistency. He says (Nirukta 11.23), ‘It may be objected, howcan it be that Dak·π£a was born from Aditi and afterwards Aditi from Dak·π£a? The answer is, either they were borntogether or by a divine law they reciprocally gave birth to each other and shared each other’s nature’ "

Jamison Brereton

The earth was born from the one whose feet were opened up; from the earth the regions of space were born.
From Aditi, Dak·π£a was born, and from Dak·π£a, Aditi.

Griffith

Earth sprang from the Productive Power the regions from the earth were born.
Daksa was born of Aditi, and Aditi was Daksa’s Child.

Geldner

Die Erde wurde von den Kauernden geboren, aus der Erde entstanden die Weltr√§ume. Von Aditi wurde Daksa geboren und von Daksa die Aditi.

Grassmann

Die Welt aus der Geb√§rerin, der Welten R√§ume aus der Welt, Dakscha entsprang aus Aditi, aus Dakscha wieder Aditi.

English translation:

‚ÄúAditi, who was your daughter, Dak·π£a, was born; after her the gods were born adorable, freed from thebonds of death.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

I.e. gave birth to (those ƒÄdityas)

Jamison Brereton

Because Aditi was born‚Äîshe who is your daughter, o Dak·π£a‚Äî
following her, the gods were born, the auspicious kin of the
immortal one.

Griffith

For Aditi, O Daksa, she who is thy Daughter, was brought forth.
After her were the blessed Gods born sharers of immortal life.

Geldner

Denn Aditi wurde geboren als deine Tochter, o Daksa. Nach ihr wurden die G√∂tter geboren, die guten Unsterblichkeitsgenossen.

Grassmann

Denn sie, die deine Tochter ist, o Dakscha, Aditi entsprang, Ihr nach entsprang der G√∂tter Schar, die seligen Unsterblichen.

‡§Ö‡§®‡§Ø‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡•á‡§£ ‡§ö‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§æ ‡§π‡•á ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§É ‡§Ö‡§¶‡§É ‡§Ö‡§Æ‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§∏‡§≤‡§ø‡§≤‡•á ‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∏‡§Ç‡§∞‡§¨‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡•Å ‡§≤‡§¨‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§É ‡§Ö‡§§‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§§ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§É ‡•§ ‚Äò ‡§Ü‡§™‡•ã ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§á‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç’ ( ‡§§‡•à. ‡§Ü. ‡•ß‡•¶. ‡•®‡•® ) ‚Äò ‡§Ö‡§™ ‡§è‡§µ ‡§∏‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§ú‡§æ‡§¶‡•å ’ (‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å.‡•ß.‡•Æ) ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡•Ä ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§∏‡§≤‡§ø‡§≤‡•á ‡§®‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§µ ‡§µ‡§É ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡•Ä ‡§§‡•Ä‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡§É ‡§¶‡•Å‡§É‡§∏‡§π‡§É ‡§∞‡•á‡§£‡•Å‡§É ‡§Ö‡§Ç‡§∂‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§ ‡§è‡§ï‡§É ‡§Ö‡§™‡§æ‡§Ø‡§§ ‡§Ö‡§™‡§æ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§§‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø ‡§ó‡§§ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‚Äò ‡§™‡§∞‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ’ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§µ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúWhen, goeds, your abode in this pool well-arranged, then a pungent dust went forth from you as if youwere dancing.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Your abode in this pool: i.e., when the world was, yet water (ƒÅpo vƒÅ idam sarvam: Taittirƒ´yaƒÄra·πáyaka: 10.22), as it was before creation, and the gods were, properly speaking, uncreated;

Dust: or,particle, this refers to the sun mounting into the sky

Jamison Brereton

When, o gods, well clasped to one another, you stood there in the ocean, then the bitter dust [=spray] dispersed from you, like (the dust [=sweat?]) of those dancing.

Griffith

When ye, O Gods, in yonder deep closeclasping one another stood,
Thence, as of dancers, from your feet a thickening cloud of dust arose.

Geldner

Als ihr G√∂tter damals in der Flut euch fest aneinanderhaltend standet, da ging von euch heftiger Staub weg wie von Tanzenden.

Grassmann

Als dort ihr standet in der Flut, o G√∂tter, wohl befestiget, Da flog von euch der dichte Staub hinweg gleichwie vom Tanzenden.

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúWhen, gods, you filled the worlds (with your radiance) as clouds (fill the earth with rain), then youbrought forth the wun hidden in the ocean.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

When, o gods, just as the Yatis did, you swelled the living worlds,
then you brought here the sun, which was hidden in the sea.

Griffith

When, O ye Gods, like Yatis, ye caused all existing things to grow,
Then ye brought Surya forward who was lying hidden in the sea.

Geldner

Als ihr G√∂tter wie Zauberer die Welten anschwelltet, da holtet ihr die im Meere versteckte Sonne.

Grassmann

Als dann wie starke Herrscher ihr, o G√∂tter, schwellen liesst die Welt, Da hobt die Sonne ihr hervor, die in dem Meer verborgen lag.

Elizarenkova

English translation:

‚ÄúEight sons (there were) of ƒÄditya who were born from her body; she approached the gods withseven, she sent forth MƒÅrtƒÅ·πá·∏ça on high (Taittirƒ´ya ƒÄra·πáyaka 1.13.3)‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Eight sons: Mitra, Varu·πáa, DhƒÅtƒÅ,Aryaman, A·πÉ≈õa, Bhaga, Vivasvat and ƒÄditya (the sun). PurƒÅ·πáƒÅs made the ƒÄdityas twelve in number;SƒÅya·πáa cites Taittirƒ´ya Sa·πÉhitƒÅ 6.5.6.1

Jamison Brereton

Eight are the sons of Aditi, which were born from her body.
With seven she went forth to the gods. She cast away the one stemming from a dead egg.

Griffith

Eight are the Sons of Adid who from her body sprang to life.
With seven she went to meet the Gods she cast Martanda far away.

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§Ö‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•á‡§É) ‡§Ü‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠ ‡§∏‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§Ö‡§ñ‡§£‡•ç‡§° ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø ‡§ï‡•á (‡§Ö‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•å ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∏‡§É) ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§Ü‡§† ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Ö‡§•‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§ø-‡§â‡§∑‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ü‡§† ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§∞ ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç (‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§É-‡§™‡§∞‡§ø ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§æ) ‡§â‡§∏ ‡§´‡•à‡§≤‡•Ä ‡§π‡•Å‡§à ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§â‡§∑‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§ü ‡§π‡•Å‡§è (‡§∏‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§≠‡§ø‡§É-‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç-‡§â‡§™‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•à‡§§‡•ç) ‡§∏‡§æ‡§§ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§∞‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§â‡§™‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à (‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§æ-‡§Ü‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§Ü‡§†‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§£‡•ç‡§° ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ï ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§∞ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ß‡§æ‡§®‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§ø‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à, ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§ü ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à ‡••‡•Æ‡••

‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§∏‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ü‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§ñ‡§£‡•ç‡§° ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø ‡§∏‡•á ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§ñ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ ‡§ó‡•ã‡§≤‡•á ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç‡•§ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ß‡§æ‡§® ‡§ó‡•ã‡§≤‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§£‡•ç‡§° ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§è‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡§É‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§â‡§∑‡§æ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§∞‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§∏ ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡••‡•Æ‡••

‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§Ö‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•á‡§É-‡§Ö‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•å ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∏‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§ñ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡•á‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§∏‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•å ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡•á‡§É-‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ-‡§â‡§∑‡§∏‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•å ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§∞‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§É (‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§É-‡§™‡§∞‡§ø ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§æ‡§É) ‡§§‡§§‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§ü‡•Ä‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§æ‡§É (‡§∏‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§≠‡§ø‡§É-‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç-‡§â‡§™‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•à‡§§‡•ç) ‡§∏‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§≠‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§ó‡•ã‡§≤‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‚Äú‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡•ã ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ã ‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä‡§§‡§ø ‡§µ‡§æ‚Äù [‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•Å‡•¶ ‡•≠‡•§‡•ß‡•§‡•ß‡•´] ‡§â‡§™‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ú‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø (‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§æ ‡§Ü‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ω‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§ü‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø ‡••‡•Æ‡••

With seven sons Aditi went forth to the ancient generation.
For procreation but also for death, she brought here again the one
stemming from a dead egg.
Two hymns to Indra (73‚Äì74) are ascribed to Gaurivƒ´ti ≈öaktyƒÅ; the first is quite com plex, the second quite straightforward.
